    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2731447107" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2731447107" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/877389-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1253306231p2/877389.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2731447107?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/877389-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/436337.Concrete_Island">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2731447107" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8359194491291481770">My favorite thing aobut living in Baltimore is the DIY culture. Perhaps it's because all the touring acts book performances in DC and Philadelphia, leaving Charm City to its own devices. At any rate, I'm proud to live in a city with a <a href='https://baltimorerockopera.org/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <b>rock opera society</b>
</a>, a <a href='https://www.baltimoreshakespearefactory.org/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <b>community Shakespeare company</b>
</a> that has staged more original pronunciation productions than any other theater in the world, and a <a href='http://www.fluidmovement.org/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <b>synchronized swimming club</b>
</a> that puts on water ballets every summer. The work is of variable quality, n</span>
  <span id="freeText8359194491291481770" style="display:none">My favorite thing aobut living in Baltimore is the DIY culture. Perhaps it's because all the touring acts book performances in DC and Philadelphia, leaving Charm City to its own devices. At any rate, I'm proud to live in a city with a <a href="https://baltimorerockopera.org/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>rock opera society</b>
</a>, a <a href="https://www.baltimoreshakespearefactory.org/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>community Shakespeare company</b>
</a> that has staged more original pronunciation productions than any other theater in the world, and a <a href="http://www.fluidmovement.org/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>synchronized swimming club</b>
</a> that puts on water ballets every summer. The work is of variable quality, natch. BROS and Fluid Movement write their own exuberant productions, which frequently have thrillingly clever bits of stagecraft or choreography, but just as frequently suffer from some painfully bad writing/acting/singing/swimming. The thing that excites me, though, and that I haven't encountered in any other city that I've lived in, is that there are so many people DOING IT FOR THEMSELVES. What an old-timey idea! Culture as something to participate in rather than something you consume; something that you make, however awkward, as opposed to something that you buy, however polished. So, DIY booster and huge Welles fan that I am, I was delighted and intrigued when a storefront near my neighborhood art house cinema was occupied recently by a group billing themselves as the <a href="https://scontent-iad3-1.xx.fbcdn.net/v/t1.0-9/52308690_2163304013730079_6415519360160366592_o.jpg?_nc_cat=110&amp;_nc_ht=scontent-iad3-1.xx&amp;oh=b028fb1c2cd098129b0a7debcafe1d42&amp;oe=5D1DD9D4" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <b>
    <i>Mercury Theater</i>
  </b>
</a>. I'd been vaguely intending to check them out for a few months, and when I saw they were staging a production of J.G. Ballard's post-modern, urban Crusoe novel, <i>Concrete Island</i>, I was on it, as a friend used to say, like a hobo on a ham sandwich.<br /><br />Or should I say chicken salad? I read <i>Concrete Island</i>, the middle novel in Ballard's "urban disaster" trilogy, for the first time in the eighties. I had learned about Ballard from a symposium that included Burroughs and Sontag, who both agreed that that Ballard was one of the few contemporary writers producing English fiction worth reading, and I embarked, as I do, on a bit of a bender that included the urban disaster books, <i>The Drowned World</i> (which begs, in these climate-crisis times, to be made into a movie) and the origin story/psychological map to the entire oeuvre, <i>Empire of the Sun</i>. When I reread <i>Concrete Island</i>, after Mercury's earnest and predictably amateurish but surprisingly evocative production, I was shocked to discover that my Vintage paperback was autographed. I had met a literary hero at the height of my fandom and had no memory of it. I drank a lot in those days, but still. I also had no memory of the novel itself apart from the basic scenario, in which an injured motorist is stranded after an accident in the interstice of a freeway interchange, and that, at some point, the hungry protagonist gets manna from heaven in the form of a chicken salad sandwich discarded by a passing driver. For decades I remembered Maitland savoring the fatty taste of the "salad cream" with which the chicken salad is bound and very little else. In the pre-Interwebs days when I read the book, I had assumed that salad cream was mayonnaise, but I now know, Google be thanked, that it is a very similar egg-and-oil emulsion (Hellman's makes a version), but looser and tarter. For what it's worth.<br /><br />The novel is written with Ballard's characteristic, cold precision. The descriptions of injury and other uncomfortable aspects of inhabiting a physical body, while not as wince-inducing as the somatic eroto-horror writing in <i>Crash</i>, put Ballard's medical education to vivid use. There is a great deal of description of the physical layout of the "island" that I found unnecessary and beyond the power of my mind's eye to keep straight. The plot and characters are evocative (deliberately, I should think) of other notable castaway narratives like<i> Robinson Crusoe</i> (of course), <i>The Tempest</i>, <i>Lord of the Flies</i> and <i>The Admirable Crichton</i>. As the narrative progresses, the plot - in particular, Maitland's interaction with the other inhabitants that he encounters on the island - feels a little programmatic. The action and dialogue arise from Ballard's fable-like concept rather than organically from the characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8359194491291481770" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2919219099" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2919219099" />
    <a title="James Tingle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66386578-james-tingle"><img alt="James Tingle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491430223p2/66386578.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2919219099?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Tingle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Tingle" href="/user/show/66386578-james-tingle">James Tingle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007287048" href="/book/show/4341306-concrete-island">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2919219099" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4150823926659241515"><br/>This was the second Ballard novel that I read and it isn't as good as The Drowned World, but I preferred it to The Atrocity Exhibition, that I think was more collected pieces than an actual novel of any sorts. This one is about an architect on his way home, crashing and landing in an urban wasteland, right in the middle of a load of intersecting motorways which pass overhead, supported by huge concrete pillars and surrounding his perimeters on all sides. He is trapped on a man made island, as ca</span>
  <span id="freeText4150823926659241515" style="display:none"><br />This was the second Ballard novel that I read and it isn't as good as The Drowned World, but I preferred it to The Atrocity Exhibition, that I think was more collected pieces than an actual novel of any sorts. This one is about an architect on his way home, crashing and landing in an urban wasteland, right in the middle of a load of intersecting motorways which pass overhead, supported by huge concrete pillars and surrounding his perimeters on all sides. He is trapped on a man made island, as cars zoom around him, beyond the steep banks of his lonely, desolate enclosure...<br />Not a lot happens in the book and it is a bit like a typical, stranded-on-a-desert-island type scenario, but instead of multi-coloured squawking parrots, palm trees and gently lapping waves, there are dead-eyed pigeons, spindly bushes and pollution filled rancid puddles. He does a lot of wandering about the place feeling helpless as he tries to figure out what the hell to do about his troubling predicament, as you'd expect, before things suddenly become a little more interesting...<br />I think it was quite an enjoyable read overall, but I feel the whole modern alienation idea and being suddenly cast adrift, while still in view of the rest of society, showing how easy it is to be disconnected in our age, was a more interesting idea than the full novel itself...still, not a bad read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4150823926659241515" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2396318867" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2396318867" />

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Radiantflux" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Radiantflux" href="/user/show/23195904-radiantflux">Radiantflux</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2396318867" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14472298192899743050">51st book for 2018.<br/><br/>In this short novel, an architect suffers a blowout on an expressway near London airport and suddenly finds himself in an industrial wasteland, from which he seemingly cannot escape. Ballard uses words sparingly, almost clinically, in this meditation on urban alienation. <br/><br/>3-stars.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3591514822" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3591514822" />
    <a title="Erik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15103969-erik"><img alt="Erik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499455982p2/15103969.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3591514822?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik" href="/user/show/15103969-erik">Erik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007287048" href="/book/show/4341306-concrete-island">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3591514822" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1609748458144720693">"Concrete Island" is J.G. Ballard's fast-paced, head scratcher of a dystopian novel.<br/><br/>Maitland finds himself wrecked on an island amongst the highways outside London, an island hidden in plain sight under overpass after overpass. As he struggles to survive with little food, an injured leg, and people who seem to constantly hinder his escape, he begins to realize his escape is not what he truly wants. Unfortunately, there is something about this book that just does not make sense - and not in a fan</span>
  <span id="freeText1609748458144720693" style="display:none">"Concrete Island" is J.G. Ballard's fast-paced, head scratcher of a dystopian novel.<br /><br />Maitland finds himself wrecked on an island amongst the highways outside London, an island hidden in plain sight under overpass after overpass. As he struggles to survive with little food, an injured leg, and people who seem to constantly hinder his escape, he begins to realize his escape is not what he truly wants. Unfortunately, there is something about this book that just does not make sense - and not in a fantasy/sci fi kind of way. The whole book is premised on being stranded on an industrial island as car after car goes by on the highway, stranded for weeks on end. And yet we are to believe that the protagonist was unable to escape. Something about this just does not make sense in even an allegorical sort of way.<br /><br />A quick read and superbly written yet not quite clear: "Concrete Island."</span>
  <a data-text-id="1609748458144720693" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FvdTNMCGPTLviWDyNzHOW0vOs64taqUHDJbvdLw3+Dbin0lRj2R6gNJ0orxRwAZ6nk8bJiM0i39zMEKirD1mbA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RJvmDDEExxk86rLft8B9JveB35vH3zrf9+4erbFMDzaw8/xpfuaAqwEXcJHRMbUHIgB3E8mBFKeISLN7oUaRbA==')})
  }

CYCLONOPEDIA is a middle-eastern Odyssey, populated by archeologists, jihadis, oil smugglers, Delta Force officers, heresiarchs, corpses of ancient gods and other puppets. The journey to the Underworld begins with petroleum basins and the rotting Sun, continuing along the tentacled pipelines of oil, and at last unfolding in the desert, where monotheism meets the Earth\'s tarry dreams of insurrection against the Sun. \'The Middle East is a sentient entity - it is alive!\' concludes renegade Iranian archeologist Dr. Hamid Parsani, before disappearing under mysterious circumstances. The disordered notes he leaves behind testify to an increasingly deranged preoccupation with oil as the \'lubricant\' of historical and political narratives. A young American woman arrives in Istanbul to meet a pseudonymous online acquaintance who never arrives. Discovering a strange manuscript in her hotel room, she follows up its cryptic clues only to discover more plot-holes, and begins to wonder whether her friend was a fictional quantity all along. Meanwhile, as the War on Terror escalates, the US is dragged into an asymmetrical engagement with occultures whose principles are ancient, obscure, and saturated in oil. Burroughs, es al mismo tiempo un descarnado autorretrato narrativo, una historia de amor brutalmente realista, una grotesca fantasía tragicómica y una ingeniosa novela política. Un libro que proporciona muchas claves fundamentales para adentrarse en el arrollador universo literario del autor. Esta edición definitiva, editada con motivo del 25 aniversario de su primera publicación, incorpora una extensa y documentada introducción de Oliver Harris en la que se repasan las complejas circunstancias personales en las que la obra fue escrita y que marcarían la vida y la posterior trayectoria literaria de Burroughs. Y se incluye también, a modo de epílogo, el texto que el propio autor escribió como prólogo para la edición de 1985.\n\n&quot;Queer&quot; está ambientada en un inmenso suburbio, que Burroughs definiría más tarde como la «Interzona», y que abarca desde la Ciudad de México, capital mundial del delito, hasta Panamá. Un álter ego del escritor, Lee, teje su tela amorosa en torno a Allerton, un joven ambiguo, indiferente como un animal. Deambula por locales cada vez más sórdidos, en los que pulula una fauna en estado de descomposición, y en esas excursiones, como un pícaro alienado, nos regala astillas radiactivas de su negrísimo humor. Para resolver sus obsesiones mortíferas y sexuales, Lee parte con su amigo a la búsqueda de la ayahuasca, droga absoluta capaz de otorgar el control total sobre los cerebros, y por eso mismo codiciada por Rusia y Estados Unidos... y por todo adicto. Dispuesto a abismarse en todos los peligros, como un santo o un criminal con orden de búsqueda y captura, Lee no tiene nada que perder. En esta novela aflora por primera vez ese paisaje alucinado que hoy todo lector reconoce como el mundo particular de William S. Burroughs.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4100104458346371489\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer own, eerily similar psychosis.\n\nA portrait of American violence that conjures the shadows of Ariel Castro, David Koresh, and Adam Lanza, Three Hundred Million is a brutal and mesmerizing masterwork,       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7010931.J_G_Ballard">About J.G. Ballard</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7010931.J_G_Ballard"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1497551843p3/7010931.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7010931.J_G_Ballard">J.G. Ballard
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
4,610
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15767970515180457339">James Graham "J. G." Ballard (15 November 1930 – 19 April 2009) was an English novelist, short story writer, and essayist. Ballard came to be associated with the New Wave of science fiction early in his career with apocalyptic (or post-apocalyptic) novels such as <i>The Drowned World</i> (1962), <i>The Burning World</i> (1964), and <i>The Crystal World</i> (1966). In the late 1960s and early 1970s Ballard focused on a</span>
  <span id="freeText15767970515180457339" style="display:none">James Graham "J. G." Ballard (15 November 1930 – 19 April 2009) was an English novelist, short story writer, and essayist. Ballard came to be associated with the New Wave of science fiction early in his career with apocalyptic (or post-apocalyptic) novels such as <i>The Drowned World</i> (1962), <i>The Burning World</i> (1964), and <i>The Crystal World</i> (1966). In the late 1960s and early 1970s Ballard focused on an eclectic variety of short stories (or "condensed novels") such as <i>The Atrocity Exhibition</i> (1970), which drew closer comparison with the work of postmodernist writers such as William S. Burroughs. In 1973 the highly controversial novel <i>Crash</i> was published, a story about symphorophilia and car crash fetishism; the protagonist becomes sexually aroused by staging and participating in real car crashes. The story was later adapted into a film of the same name by Canadian director David Cronenberg.<br /><br />While many of Ballard's stories are thematically and narratively unusual, he is perhaps best known for his relatively conventional war novel, <i>Empire of the Sun</i> (1984), a semi-autobiographical account of a young boy's experiences in Shanghai during the Second Sino-Japanese War as it came to be occupied by the Japanese Imperial Army. Described as "The best British novel about the Second World War" by <i>The Guardian</i>, the story was adapted into a 1987 film by Steven Spielberg.<br /><br />The literary distinctiveness of Ballard's work has given rise to the adjective "Ballardian", defined by the <i>Collins English Dictionary</i> as "resembling or suggestive of the conditions described in J. G. Ballard's novels and stories, especially dystopian modernity, bleak man-made landscapes and the psychological effects of technological, social or environmental developments." The <i>Oxford Dictionary of National Biography</i> entry describes Ballard's work as being occupied with "eros, thanatos, mass media and emergent technologies".</span>
  <a data-text-id="15767970515180457339" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1232126-concrete-island">Quotes from Concrete Island</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;He reached the foot of the embankment, and waved with one arm, shouting at the few cars moving along the westbound carriageway. None of the drivers could see him, let alone hear his dry-throated croak, and Maitland stopped, conserving his strength. lie tried to climb the embankment, but within a few steps collapsed in a heap on the muddy slope.<br />Deliberately, he turned his back to the motorway and for the first time began to inspect the island.<br />'Maitland, poor man, you're marooned here like Crusoe - If you don't look out you'll be beached here for ever...'<br />He had spoken no more than the truth. This patch of abandoned ground left over at the junction of three motorway routes was literally a deserted island. Angry with himself, Maitland lifted the crutch to strike this meaningless soil.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/9704295-he-reached-the-foot-of-the-embankment-and-waved-with">7 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Soon after three o'clock on the afternoon of April 22nd 1973, a 35-year-old architect named Robert Maitland was driving down the high-speed exit lane of the Westway interchange in central London. Six hundred yards from the junction with the newly built spur of the M4 motorway, when the Jaguar had already passed the 70 m.p.h. speed limit, a blow-out collapsed the front nearside tyre. The exploding air reflected from the concrete parapet seemed to detonate inside Robert Maitland's skull. During the few seconds before his crash he clutched at the whiplashing spokes of the steering wheel, dazed by the impact of the chromium window pillar against his head. The car veered from side to side across the empty traffic lanes, jerking his hands like a puppet's. The shredding tyre laid a black diagonal stroke across the white marker lines that followed the long curve of the motorway embankment. Out of control, the car burst through the palisade of pinewood trestles that formed a temporary barrier along the edge of the road. Leaving the hard shoulder, the car plunged down the grass slope of the embankment. Thirty yards ahead, it came to a halt against the rusting chassis of an overturned taxi. Barely injured by this violent tangent that had grazed his life, Robert Maitland lay across his steering wheel, his jacket and trousers studded with windshield fragments like a suit of lights.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/9704293-soon-after-three-o-clock-on-the-afternoon-of-april-22nd">6 likes</a>
            </nobr>
